Resolution of Nonsingularities, Point-theoreticity,
and Metric-admissibility for p-adic Hyperbolic
Curves
Shinichi Mochizuki and Shota Tsujimura
June 16, 2023
Abstract
In this paper, we prove that arbitrary hyperbolic curves over p-adic local ï¬elds admit resolution of nonsingularities [â€œRNSâ€]. This result may be
regarded as a generalization of results concerning resolution of nonsingularities obtained by A. Tamagawa and E. Lepage. Moreover, by combining
our RNS result with techniques from combinatorial anabelian geometry,
we prove that an absolute version of the geometrically pro-Î£ Grothendieck
Conjecture for arbitrary hyperbolic curves over p-adic local ï¬elds, where
Î£ denotes a set of prime numbers of cardinality â‰¥ 2 that contains p,
holds. This settles one of the major open questions in anabelian geometry.
Furthermore, we prove â€” again by applying RNS and combinatorial anabelian geometry â€” that the various p-adic versions of the GrothendieckTeichmuÌˆller group that appear in the literature in fact coincide. As a
corollary, we conclude that the metrized Grothendieck-TeichmuÌˆller group
is commensurably terminal in the Grothendieck-TeichmuÌˆller group. This
settles a longstanding open question in combinatorial anabelian geometry.

Our ï¬rst main result may be regarded as a generalization of these results [cf.
Theorem 2.17]:
Theorem A (Resolution of nonsingularities for arbitrary hyperbolic
curves over p-adic local ï¬elds). Suppose that Î£ âŠ† Primes is a subset of
cardinality â‰¥ 2 that contains p, and that K is a p-adic local ï¬eld. Let X be a
hyperbolic curve over K; L a mixed characteristic complete discrete valuation
ï¬eld of residue characteristic p that contains K as a topological subï¬eld. Then
XL satisï¬es Î£-RNS if and only if the residue ï¬eld of L is algebraic over the
ï¬nite ï¬eld of cardinality p.
In the remainder of the present Introduction, we discuss various anabelian
applications/consequences of Theorem A.

Next, we consider applications of Theorem A to Grothendieck Conjecturetype results in anabelian geometry. We begin by recalling the following question,
which may may be considered as an absolute version of the Grothendieck Conjecture for hyperbolic curves over p-adic local ï¬elds:

This question may be regarded as one of the major open questions in anabelian
geometry. In this context, we recall that, in the case of the relative version of
the Grothendieck Conjecture for arbitrary hyperbolic curves, many satisfactory
results have been obtained [cf. [PrfGC], Theorem A; [Tama1], Theorem 0.4;
[LocAn], Theorem A]. In particular, the ï¬rst author of the present paper gave a
complete aï¬ƒrmative answer to the original question posed by A. Grothendieck
[i.e., the original â€œGrothendieck Conjectureâ€] in quite substantial generality [cf.
[LocAn], Theorem A; [AnabTop], Theorem 4.12]. On the other hand, in the case

Finally, we conclude with an interesting complement to the theory of p-adic
arithmetic cuspidalizations by applying Theorem C, together with the theory
of metric-admissibility developed in [CbTpIII], Â§3, to construct certain p-adic
arithmetic cuspidalizations of the geometric tempered fundamental group of a
hyperbolic curve over Qp equipped with certain relatively mild auxiliary data
[cf. Deï¬nition 3.19; Theorem 3.20; Remarks 3.20.1, 3.20.2].
The contents of the present paper may be summarized as follows:
In Â§1, we discuss in detail certain local computations â€” motivated by [Lpg1],
Proposition 2.4, but formulated entirely in the language of schemes and formal schemes, i.e., without resorting to the use of notions from the theory of
Berkovich spaces â€” concerning iterates of the p-th power morphism of the multiplicative group scheme Gm over the ring of integers of a mixed characteristic
discrete valuation ï¬eld of residue characteristic p. By restricting such morphisms to suitable formal neighborhoods, we conclude that smooth curves of
genus â‰¥ 1 appear in the special ï¬bers of suitable models of the domain curves
of such morphisms, i.e., as certain Artin-Schreier coverings of curves of genus 0
[cf. Proposition 1.6; Remark 1.6.2].
In Â§2, we begin by discussing various generalities concerning models of a
hyperbolic curve over a mixed characteristic complete discrete valuation ï¬eld
[cf. Deï¬nition 2.1; Proposition 2.3]. In particular, we discuss the deï¬nition of
the notion of Î£-RNS [cf. Deï¬nition 2.2, (vii)], together with closely related basic
properties of this notion [cf. Propositions 2.4; Corollary 2.5]. Another important
9

notion in this context is the purely combinatorial notion of VE-chains associated
to a hyperbolic curve over a mixed characteristic complete discrete valuation
ï¬eld [cf. Deï¬nition 2.2, (iii)]. This notion is closely related to the topological
Berkovich space associated to the hyperbolic curve [cf. Proposition 2.3, (vii),
(viii)]. Finally, we apply certain constructions involving p-divisible groups to
extend the Artin-Schreier coverings constructed locally in Â§1 to coverings of an
arbitrary hyperbolic curve over a p-adic local ï¬eld [cf. Propositions 2.12, 2.13;
Theorem 2.16]. This leads naturally to a proof of Theorem A [cf. Theorem 2.17],
hence also, by combining Theorem A with the theory of VE-chains developed in
the earlier portion of Â§2, together with some combinatorial anabelian geometry,
of Theorem B.
In Â§3, we begin by recalling the well-known classiï¬cation of the points of
the topological Berkovich space associated to a proper hyperbolic curve over a
mixed characteristic complete discrete valuation ï¬eld via the notion of type i
points, where i âˆˆ {1, 2, 3, 4}. Next, we introduce a certain combinatorial classiï¬cation of the VE-chains considered in Â§2 and observe that this classiï¬cation
of VE-chains leads naturally to a purely combinatorial characterization of the
well-known classiï¬cation via type i points mentioned above. We then apply the
theory of Â§2 to give a group-theoretic characterization, motivated by [but by no
means identical to] the characterization of [Lpg2], Â§4, of the type i points in
terms of the geometric Î£-tempered fundamental group of the hyperbolic curve.
The theory surrounding this group-theoretic characterization leads naturally
to proofs of Theorems C and F. Moreover, by combining this group-theoretic
characterization with [AbsTopII], Corollary 2.9; [HMM], Theorem A, we obtain
proofs of Theorems D and E [cf. Theorems 3.12; 3.13]. We then switch gears
to discuss metric-admissibility for p-adic hyperbolic curves. This discussion
of metric-admissibility yields, in particular, a proof of Theorem G and leads
naturally to the discussion of p-adic arithmetic cuspidalizations associated to
geometric tempered fundamental groups equipped with certain relatively mild
auxiliary data [cf. Deï¬nition 3.19; Theorem 3.20; Remarks 3.20.1, 3.20.2] mentioned above.

10

Notations and Conventions
Numbers:
The notation Primes will be used to denote the set of prime numbers. The
notation N will be used to denote the set of nonnegative integers. The notation
Qâ‰¥0 will be used to denote the additive monoid of nonnegative rational numbers.
The notation Z will be used to denote the additive group or ring of integers. The
notation Q will be used to denote the ï¬eld of rational numbers. The notation
R will be used to denote the ï¬eld of real numbers. For each x âˆˆ R, the notation
x will be used to denote the greatest integer â‰¤ x. If p is a prime number,
then the notation Qp will be used to denote the ï¬eld of p-adic numbers; the
notation Zp will be used to denote the additive group or ring of p-adic integers;
the notation Qp will be used to denote an algebraic closure of Qp ; the notation
Cp will be used to denote the p-adic completion of Qp . We shall refer to a ï¬nite
extension ï¬eld of Qp as a p-adic local ï¬eld.
Monoids:
Let M be a commutative monoid. In this subsection, we regard the set
of positive integers Nâ‰¥1 as a directed set via its multiplicative structure, i.e.,
def

i â‰¤ j â‡” i | j. For i âˆˆ Nâ‰¥1 , write Mi = M . For i, j âˆˆ Nâ‰¥1 such that
i â‰¤ j, write Ï†i,j : Mi â†’ Mj for the homomorphism of monoids obtained by
multiplication by ji . Then we shall refer to the inductive limit of the inductive
system {Mi , Ï†i,j } on the directed set Nâ‰¥1 as the perfection of M .
Rings and ï¬elds:
Let R be a ring. Then we shall write RÃ— for the multiplicative group of
units of the ring.
Let F be a perfect ï¬eld, p a prime number. Then the notation F will be
def
used to denote an algebraic closure of F ; GF = Gal(F /F ).
Suppose that F is a valuation ï¬eld, i.e., a ï¬eld equipped with a valuation
map [cf., e.g., the axioms of [EP], Eq. (2.1.2)]. Then we shall write OF for the
ring of integers of F ; mF for the maximal ideal of OF . Thus, the valuation map
on F induces an isomorphism of ordered abelian groups between F Ã— /OFÃ— and
the value group of the valuation ï¬eld F . In particular, any valuation map on
the ï¬eld F is determined, up to unique isomorphism [in the evident sense], by
the ring of integers âŠ† F associated to the valuation map. In the present paper,
we shall use the term valuation to refer to an isomorphism class of
valuation maps on a ï¬eld F , i.e., the collection of valuation maps
that give rise to the same ring of integers âŠ† F .
We shall refer to a speciï¬c valuation map within a given isomorphism class of
valuation maps on a ï¬eld as a normalized valuation, i.e., an isomorphism class of
valuation maps on the ï¬eld, together with a speciï¬c valuation map [belonging to
this class], which we shall refer to as a normalization. If the value group of the

11

valuation ï¬eld F is isomorphic, as an ordered abelian group, to a subgroup of
the underlying additive group of R, then we shall say that F is a real valuation
ï¬eld. If F is a mixed characteristic real valuation ï¬eld of residue characteristic
p, then the notation vp will be used to denote the normalized valuation on F
whose normalization is determined by the condition that vp (p) = 1 âˆˆ R. If F is
a henselian valuation ï¬eld of characteristic 0, then we shall write IF âŠ† GF for
the inertia subgroup; F âŠ† F ur (âŠ† F ) for the maximal unramiï¬ed extension. If
F is a real henselian valuation ï¬eld of characteristic 0, then we shall write F ur
for the completion of F ur [cf. Remark 2.2.4 for more details].

1

Local construction of Artin-Schreier extensions
in the special ï¬ber

Let p be a prime number. In the present section, we perform various local
computations concerning iterates of the p-th power morphism of the multiplicative group scheme Gm over the ring of integers of a mixed characteristic discrete
valuation ï¬eld of residue characteristic p. As a consequence, by restricting such
morphisms to suitable formal neighborhoods, we conclude that smooth curves
of genus â‰¥ 1 appear in the special ï¬bers of suitable models of the domain curves
of such morphisms [cf. Proposition 1.6; Remark 1.6.2]. This observation will be
applied in Â§2 to prove that arbitrary hyperbolic curves over p-adic local ï¬elds admit resolution of nonsingularities. The contents of this section may be regarded
as an alternative and somewhat more detailed discussion of [Lpg1], Proposition
2.4, that is phrased entirely in the language of schemes and formal schemes and
does not resort to the use of Berkovich spaces.
First, we begin with several elementary lemmas concerning the p-adic valuations of the coeï¬ƒcients of certain polynomials and power series [cf. Lemmas
1.1, 1.2, 1.3].

Resolution of nonsingularities for arbitrary hyperbolic curves over p-adic local ï¬elds

Let p be a prime number. In the present section, we apply certain constructions involving p-divisible groups to extend the Artin-Schreier coverings
constructed locally in Â§1 to coverings of an arbitrary hyperbolic curve. As a
consequence, we prove that arbitrary hyperbolic curves over p-adic local ï¬elds
satisfy RNS, i.e., â€œresolution of nonsingularitiesâ€ [cf. Deï¬nition 2.2, (vii); Theorem 2.17]. This result may be regarded as a generalization of results obtained
by A. Tamagawa and E. Lepage [cf. [Tama2], Theorem 0.2; [Lpg1], Theorem
2.7]. Historically [cf., e.g., the discussion in the Introduction to [Tama2]], the
roots of these results of Tamagawa and Lepage may be traced back to the technique of â€œpassing to a covering with singular reduction of a given curve with
smooth reduction over a p-adic local ï¬eldâ€ applied in the proof of [PrfGC], Theorem 9.2 [cf. also Proposition 2.3, (xii), below]. Moreover, the techniques of
[AbsTopII], Â§2, may be regarded as a sort of weak, pro-p version of Tamagawaâ€™s
RNS [cf. [AbsTopII], Remark 2.6.1]. In fact, the approach of the present section may be regarded as a sort of amalgamation of the techniques of [Lpg1]
with the techniques of [AbsTopII], Â§2. At any rate, from a historical point of
view, it is interesting to observe how various RNS results have been motivated
by and indeed are deeply intertwined with various results in anabelian geometry
[cf. Corollary 2.5, as well as Theorems 3.12, 3.13 in Â§3 below].
First, we begin by ï¬xing our conventions concerning models of hyperbolic
curves [cf. [DM], Deï¬nition 1.1; [Knud], Deï¬nition 1.1].
Deï¬nition 2.1. Let K be a valuation ï¬eld; X a hyperbolic curve over K; X a
scheme over OK . Then:
(i) We shall say that X is a compactiï¬ed model of X over OK if X is a proper,
ï¬‚at, normal scheme of ï¬nite presentation over OK whose generic ï¬ber is
the [uniquely determined, up to unique isomorphism] smooth compactiï¬cation of X over K.
(ii) Suppose that the cusps of X are K-rational. Then we shall say that X
is a compactiï¬ed semistable model of X over OK if X is a compactiï¬ed
model of X over OK such that the following conditions hold:
â€¢ the geometric special ï¬ber of X is a semistable curve [i.e., a reduced,
connected curve each of whose nonsmooth points is an ordinary double point];
â€¢ the images of the sections Spec OK â†’ X determined by the cusps of
X [which we shall refer to as cusps of X ] lie in the smooth locus of
X and do not intersect each other.
Suppose that X is a compactiï¬ed semistable model of X over OK . Then
we shall say that X has split reduction if Xs is split [i.e., each of the
irreducible components and nodes of Xs is geometrically irreducible].
26

(iii) Suppose that X is a compactiï¬ed semistable model of X over OK that has
split reduction. Let L be a ï¬nite extension of K equipped with a valuation
that extends the valuation on K; X âˆ— a compactiï¬ed semistable model of
XL over OL such that X âˆ— has split reduction and dominates X . Then we

to an irreducible component of Xsâˆ— that maps to a closed point of X .
(iv) We shall say that X is a compactiï¬ed stable model of X over OK if X is a
compactiï¬ed semistable model of X over OK such that X , together with
the cusps of X , determines a pointed stable curve.
(v) We shall say that X is a semistable model of X over OK if X is obtained

Theorem, up to unique isomorphism] compactiï¬ed semistable model of X
over OK . Suppose that X is a semistable model of X over OK . Then we
shall say that X has split reduction if Xs is split [i.e., each of the irreducible
components and nodes of Xs is geometrically irreducible].
(vi) We shall say that X is a stable model of X over OK if X is obtained
by removing the cusps from a [uniquely determined, by Zariskiâ€™s Main
Theorem, up to unique isomorphism] compactiï¬ed stable model of X over
OK .
(vii) We shall say that X has stable reduction over K if there exists a [necessarily
unique, up to unique isomorphism] stable model of X over OK . Suppose
that X is a stable model of X over OK . Then we shall say that X has
split stable reduction over K if Xs is split [i.e., each of the irreducible
components and nodes of Xs is geometrically irreducible].

complete discrete valuation ï¬eld.

The various basic properties stated in the following Proposition 2.3 consist
of elementary results that are essentially well-known or implicit in the literature
[cf. Remarks 2.3.2, 2.3.3 below], but we give [essentially] self-contained statements and proofs here in the language of the present discussion for the sake of
completeness.

Theorem 2.17 (Resolution of nonsingularities for arbitrary hyperbolic
curves over p-adic local ï¬elds). Let Î£ âŠ† Primes be a subset of cardinality
â‰¥ 2; K a p-adic local ï¬eld, for some p âˆˆ Î£; X a hyperbolic curve over K; L a
mixed characteristic complete discrete valuation ï¬eld of residue characteristic p
that contains K as a topological subï¬eld. Then XL satisï¬es Î£-RNS if and only
if the residue ï¬eld of L is algebraic over the ï¬nite ï¬eld of cardinality p.
Proof. First, we observe that it follows formally from Remark 2.2.3, (v), that
it suï¬ƒces to verify that X satisï¬es Î£-RNS. Next, we observe that it follows
71

immediately from the various deï¬nitions involved that we may assume without
loss of generality that X has stable reduction over K. Write X for the [unique,
up to unique isomorphism] compactiï¬ed stable model of X over OK . Then,
in light of Propositions 2.3, (xii); 2.4, (i), (ii), by replacing X by the [unique,
up to unique isomorphism] smooth compactiï¬cation of a suitable connected
geometrically pro-Î£ ï¬nite eÌtale covering of X, we may assume without loss of
generality that:
â€¢ X is a proper hyperbolic curve over K,
â€¢ Xs is split,
â€¢ Xs is singular, and
â€¢ every irreducible component of Xs is a smooth curve of genus â‰¥ 2.
In particular, X now satisï¬es the assumptions imposed in the respective initial
portions of Proposition 2.6 and Deï¬nition 2.7. Next, observe that since the
covering Yn â†’ Y is combinatorial [cf. Deï¬nition 2.7, (ii)], it follows immediately
that this covering induces a surjection Yn (K)  Y (K) on K-rational points.
Thus, it follows immediately from Proposition 2.4, (iii), and Theorem 2.16 that
X satisï¬es Î£-RNS. This completes the proof of Theorem 2.17.

3

Point-theoreticity, metric-admissibility, and arithmetic cuspidalization

Let p be a prime number. In the present section, we ï¬rst recall the wellknown classiï¬cation of the points of the topological Berkovich space associated
to a proper hyperbolic curve over a mixed characteristic complete discrete valuation ï¬eld via the notion of type i points, where i âˆˆ {1, 2, 3, 4} [cf. Deï¬nition 3.1]. Next, we introduce a certain combinatorial classiï¬cation of the
VE-chains considered in Â§2 [cf. Deï¬nition 3.2] and observe that this classiï¬cation of VE-chains leads naturally to a purely combinatorial characterization of
the well-known classiï¬cation via type i points mentioned above [cf. Propositions 3.3, 3.4]. This combinatorial classiï¬cation/characterization [cf. also the
approach of Propositions 3.7, 3.8] was motivated by the argument applied in
the proof of [CbTpIV], Theorem A.7. We then apply the theory of Â§2 to give
a group-theoretic characterization, motivated by [but by no means identical to]
the characterization of [Lpg2], Â§4, of the type i points in terms of the geometric
Î£-tempered fundamental group of the hyperbolic curve [cf. Propositions 3.5,
3.9]. Then, by combining this group-theoretic characterization with [AbsTopII],
Corollary 2.9, we prove an absolute version of the Grothendieck Conjecture for
hyperbolic curves over p-adic local ï¬elds [cf. Theorem 3.12]. This settles one of
the major open questions in anabelian geometry. As a corollary of this absolute
version of the Grothendieck Conjecture for hyperbolic curves over p-adic local
72

ï¬elds, together with [HMM], Theorem A, we also obtain an absolute version
of the Grothendieck Conjecture for conï¬guration spaces associated to hyperbolic curves over p-adic local ï¬elds [cf. Theorem 3.13]. We then switch gears
to discuss metric-admissibility for p-adic hyperbolic curves. This discussion of
metric-admissibility leads to a proof that all of the various p-adic versions of
the Grothendieck-TeichmuÌˆller group that appear in the literature in fact coincide [cf. Theorem 3.16]. Moreover, as an application of Corollary 2.5, (i), and
the theory developed in the present section, together with the theory of metricadmissibility developed in [CbTpIII], Â§3, we obtain a construction of a certain
type of arithmetic cuspidalization of the [Primes-] tempered fundamental group
of a hyperbolic curve over Qp [cf. Theorem 3.20].

We are now in a position to verify an absolute version of the Grothendieck
